Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Tmazight" class="mw-redirect" title="Tmazight">Tmazight</a>.</div>

class="infobox-above" style="background-color:  #faecc8;">Berber</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-subheader" style="background-color:  #faecc8;">Tamazight<br />Amazigh<br /><span Other areas, especially in North Africa, contain minority Berber-speaking populations.<br /><div>

<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<p><b>Northern Berber</b>
</p><p><i>Kabyle</i>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#5D0295; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160; <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a> (Taqbaylit)</div>
<p><i>Atlas</i>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#BB2215; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Central_Atlas_Tamazight" title="Central Atlas Tamazight">Central Atlas Tamazight</a> (Tamaziɣt)</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF6A73; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Shilha_language" title="Shilha language">Shilha</a> (Tacelḥit) </div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#84003F; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Sanhaja_de_Srair_language" title="Sanhaja de Srair language">Senhaja de Srair</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Ghomara_language" title="Ghomara language">Ghomara</a></div>
<p><i>Zenati</i>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#7FE4B3; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tarifit" title="Tarifit">Tarifit</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#AFF5B6; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Ait_Seghrouchen_Berber" title="Ait Seghrouchen Berber">Ayt Seghrouchen</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Middle_Atlas_Berber" title="Eastern Middle Atlas Berber">Ayt Warayn</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#308D2E; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Shenwa_language" title="Shenwa language">Shenwa</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00571E; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Shawiya_language" title="Shawiya language">Shawiya</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#C4F361; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;Other Zenati (<a href="/wiki/Mzab%E2%80%93Wargla_languages" title="Mzab–Wargla languages">Mzab-Wargla</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Zenati_languages" title="East Zenati languages">East Zenati</a>)</div>
</td>
<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<p><b>Western Berber</b>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#034665; color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Zenaga_language" title="Zenaga language">Zenaga</a> (Tuḍḍungiyya)</div>
<p><b>Eastern Berber</b>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFD873; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Berber_languages" title="Eastern Berber languages">Eastern Berber</a> (Siwi, Nafusi, Sokna, Ghadamès, Awjila)</div>
<p><b>Tuareg</b>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#6495ED; color:black;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tuareg</a> (Tamasheq)</div>
<p>&#32;
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1134653256">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Yakh.svg/27px-Yakh.svg.png" decoding="async" width="27" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Yakh.svg/41px-Yakh.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Yakh.svg/55px-Yakh.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="878" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support_(Tifinagh)" class="mw-redirect" title="Help:Multilingual support (Tifinagh)">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh letters</a>.</div></div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Berber languages</b>, also known as the <b>Amazigh languages</b> or <b>Tamazight</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> are a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_language_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Afroasiatic language family">Afroasiatic language family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> They comprise a group of closely related but mostly <a href="/wiki/Mutually_unintelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually unintelligible">mutually unintelligible</a> languages<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> spoken by <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> communities, who are indigenous to <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hayward-2000_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayward-2000-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brett_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brett-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The languages are primarily spoken and not typically written.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-2012b-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Historically, they have been written with the ancient <a href="/wiki/Libyco-Berber" class="mw-redirect" title="Libyco-Berber">Libyco-Berber</a> script, which now exists in the form of <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Punic_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Punic-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Today, they may also be written in the <a href="/wiki/Berber_Latin_alphabet" title="Berber Latin alphabet">Berber Latin alphabet</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>, with Latin being the most pervasive.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-choice_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-choice-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Silverstein-2004_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silverstein-2004-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Berber languages have a similar level of variety to the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, although they are sometimes referred to as a single collective language, often as "Berber", "Tamazight", or "Amazigh".<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020b_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020b-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ridouane-2014_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ridouane-2014-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekkehard_Wolff-2013_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekkehard_Wolff-2013-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peab_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peab-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The languages, with a few exceptions, form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020b_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020b-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> There is a debate as to how to best sub-categorize languages within the Berber branch.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020b_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020b-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012a_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012a-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Berber languages typically follow <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object_word_order" title="Verb–subject–object word order">verb–subject–object word order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mettouchi-2010_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mettouchi-2010-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Their phonological inventories are diverse.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012a_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012a-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Millions of people in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> natively speak a Berber language, as do smaller populations of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, western and northern <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa Oasis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddy-Weitzman-2011b_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddy-Weitzman-2011b-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> There are also likely a few million speakers of Berber languages in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shilha_language" title="Shilha language">Tashlhiyt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Atlas_Tamazight" title="Central Atlas Tamazight">Central Atlas Tamazight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riffian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Riffian language">Tarifit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shawiya_language" title="Shawiya language">Shawiya</a> are some of the most commonly spoken Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Maddy-Weitzman-2011b_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddy-Weitzman-2011b-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Exact numbers are impossible to ascertain as there are few modern North African censuses that include questions on language use, and what censuses do exist have known flaws.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013d_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013d-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following independence in the 20th century, the Berber languages have been suppressed and suffered from low <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">prestige</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013d_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013d-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Recognition of the Berber languages has been growing in the 21st century, with Morocco and Algeria adding Tamazight as an official language to their constitutions in 2011 and 2016 respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013d_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013d-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Madanin-2012_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madanin-2012-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News-2016_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-2016-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Berber languages have a high percentage of borrowing and influence from the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic language</a>, as well as from other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> For example, Arabic loanwords represent 35%<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> to 46%<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> of the total vocabulary of the <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle language</a> and represent 51.7% of the total vocabulary of <a href="/wiki/Tarifit" title="Tarifit">Tarifit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Almost all Berber languages took from Arabic the <a href="/wiki/Voiced_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiced pharyngeal fricative">pharyngeal fricatives</a> /ʕ/ and /ħ/, the (nongeminated) uvular stop /q/, and the voiceless <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealized consonant</a> /ṣ/.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Berber_people" title="Names of the Berber people">Names of the Berber people</a></div><p>"Tamazight" and "Berber languages" are often used interchangeably.<sup id="cite_ref-Ridouane-2014_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ridouane-2014-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ekkehard_Wolff-2013_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ekkehard_Wolff-2013-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> However, "Tamazight" is sometimes used to refer to a specific subset of Berber languages, such as Central Tashlhiyt.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> "Tamazight" can also be used to refer to <a href="/wiki/Standard_Moroccan_Amazigh" title="Standard Moroccan Amazigh">Standard Moroccan Tamazight</a> or <a href="/wiki/Standard_Algerian_Berber" title="Standard Algerian Berber">Standard Algerian Tamazight</a>, as in the Moroccan and Algerian constitutions respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_bill-2016_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution_bill-2016-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In Morocco, besides referring to all Berber languages or to Standard Moroccan Tamazight, "Tamazight" is often used in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Shilha_language" title="Shilha language">Tashelhit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tarifit" title="Tarifit">Tarifit</a> to refer to <a href="/wiki/Central_Atlas_Tamazight" title="Central Atlas Tamazight">Central Atlas Tamazight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of <i>Berber</i> has been the subject of debate due to its historical background as an <a href="/wiki/Endonym_and_exonym" title="Endonym and exonym">exonym</a> and present equivalence with the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> word for "barbarian."<sup id="cite_ref-Maddy-Weitzman-2011a2_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddy-Weitzman-2011a2-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vourlias-2010a2_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vourlias-2010a2-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Block-20192_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Block-20192-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cheref-20212_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheref-20212-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> One group, the <a href="/wiki/Linguasphere_Observatory" title="Linguasphere Observatory">Linguasphere Observatory</a>, has attempted to introduce the <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologism</a> "Tamazic languages" to refer to the Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Amazigh people typically use "Tamazight" when speaking English.<sup id="cite_ref-Brenzinger-20152_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brenzinger-20152-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Historically, Berbers did not refer to themselves as Berbers/Amazigh but had their own terms to refer to themselves. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_people" title="Kabyle people">Kabyles</a> use the term "Leqbayel" to refer to their own people, while the <a href="/wiki/Chaoui_people" title="Chaoui people">Chaouis</a> identified themselves as "Ishawiyen" instead of Berber/Amazigh.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Berber_language" title="Proto-Berber language">Proto-Berber language</a></div>
<p>Since modern Berber languages are relatively homogeneous, the date of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Berber_language" title="Proto-Berber language">Proto-Berber language</a> from which the modern group is derived was probably comparatively recent, comparable to the age of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance</a> subfamilies of the Indo-European family. In contrast, the split of the group from the other Afroasiatic sub-phyla is much earlier, and is therefore sometimes associated with the local <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Capsian_culture" title="Capsian culture">Capsian culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A number of extinct populations are believed to have spoken Afroasiatic languages of the Berber branch. According to Peter Behrens and Marianne Bechaus-Gerst, linguistic evidence suggests that the peoples of the <a href="/wiki/C-Group_culture" title="C-Group culture">C-Group culture</a> in present-day southern <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> spoke Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Bechaus-Gerst_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bechaus-Gerst-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lbant_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lbant-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a> <a href="/wiki/Nobiin_language" title="Nobiin language">Nobiin language</a> today contains a number of key <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> related to <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoralism</a> that are of Berber origin, including the terms for sheep and water/<a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. This in turn suggests that the C-Group population—which, along with the <a href="/wiki/Kerma_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerma Culture">Kerma culture</a>, inhabited the Nile valley immediately before the arrival of the first <a href="/wiki/Nubian_languages" title="Nubian languages">Nubian</a> speakers—spoke Afroasiatic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Bechaus-Gerst_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bechaus-Gerst-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Orthography"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Berber_orthography" title="Berber orthography">Berber orthography</a></div><figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Prehistory-draa16.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Prehistory-draa16.jpg/220px-Prehistory-draa16.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Prehistory-draa16.jpg/330px-Prehistory-draa16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Prehistory-draa16.jpg/440px-Prehistory-draa16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="634" data-file-height="477" /></a><figcaption>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Libyco-Berber</a> inscriptions in <a href="/wiki/Zagora,_Morocco" title="Zagora, Morocco">Zagora</a>, Morocco</figcaption></figure><p>Berber languages are primarily oral languages without a major written component.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-2012b-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Historically, they were written with the <a href="/wiki/Libyco-Berber_alphabet" title="Libyco-Berber alphabet">Libyco-Berber script</a>. Early uses of the script have been found on rock art and in various sepulchres; the oldest known variations of the script dates to inscriptions in <a href="/wiki/Dougga" title="Dougga">Dugga</a> from 600 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-2012b-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell-2013_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-2013-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Usage of this script, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh</a>, has continued into the present day among the <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Elmedlaoui-2012a_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elmedlaoui-2012a-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Following the spread of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, some Berber scholars also utilized the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sec14_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sec14-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Berber_Latin_alphabet" title="Berber Latin alphabet">Berber Latin alphabet</a> was developed following the introduction of the Latin script in the nineteenth century by the West.<sup id="cite_ref-Elmedlaoui-2012a_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elmedlaoui-2012a-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The nineteenth century also saw the development of Neo-Tifinagh, an adaptation of Tuareg Tifinagh for use with other Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-2012b-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aïtel-2014_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aïtel-2014-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maddy-Weitzman-2011c_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maddy-Weitzman-2011c-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are now three writing systems in use for Berber languages: Tifinagh, the Arabic script, and the <a href="/wiki/Berber_Latin_alphabet" title="Berber Latin alphabet">Berber Latin alphabet</a>, with the Latin alphabet being the most widely used today.<sup id="cite_ref-choice_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-choice-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Silverstein-2004_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Silverstein-2004-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subclassification">Subclassification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Subclassification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>
With the exception of Zenaga, Tetserret, and Tuareg, the Berber languages form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>. Different linguists take different approaches towards drawing boundaries between languages in this continuum.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020b_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020b-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Maarten Kossmann notes that it is difficult to apply the classic <a href="/wiki/Tree_model" title="Tree model">tree model</a> of historical linguistics towards the Berber languages:</p><blockquote><p>[The Berber language family]'s continuous history of convergence and differentiation along new lines makes an definition of branches arbitrary. Moreover, mutual intelligibility and mutual influence render notions such as "split" or "branching" rather difficult to apply except, maybe, in the case of Zenaga and Tuareg.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020a_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020a-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Kossmann roughly groups the Berber languages into seven blocks:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020a_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020a-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1116488514">.mw-parser-output .treeview ul{padding:0;margin:0}.mw-parser-output .treeview li{padding:0;margin:0;list-style-type:none;list-style-image:none}.mw-parser-output .treeview li li{background:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Treeview-grey-line.png")no-repeat 0 -2981px;padding-left:21px;text-indent:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .treeview li li:last-child{background-position:0 -5971px}.mw-parser-output .treeview li.emptyline>ul>.mw-empty-elt:first-child+.emptyline,.mw-parser-output .treeview li.emptyline>ul>li:first-child{background-position:0 9px}</style></p><div class="treeview">
<ul><li><b>Berber</b>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Western_Berber_languages" title="Western Berber languages">Western</a> (Zenaga, Tetserret)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tuareg</a></li>
<li>Western Moroccan
<ul><li>southwestern and central Moroccan languages (Tashelhiyt, most of Central Atlas Tamazight)</li>
<li>northwestern Moroccan languages (Ghomara, Senhadja de Sraïr)</li></ul></li>
<li>Zenatic (a dialect continuum stretching from eastern Morocco to the <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa Oasis</a>)</li>
<li>Kabyle</li>
<li>Ghadames</li>
<li>Awjila</li></ul></li></ul>
</div>
<p>The Zenatic block is typically divided into the <a href="/wiki/Zenati_languages" title="Zenati languages">Zenati</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Berber_languages" title="Eastern Berber languages">Eastern Berber</a> branches, due to the marked difference in features at each end of the continuum.<sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2020a_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2020a-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blažek-2010_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blažek-2010-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, subclassifications by different linguists typically combine various blocks into different branches. Western Moroccan languages, Zenati languages, Kabyle, and Ghadames may be grouped under <a href="/wiki/Northern_Berber_languages" title="Northern Berber languages">Northern Berber</a>; Awjila is often included as an Eastern Berber language alongside Siwa, Sokna, and El Foqaha. These approaches divide the Berber languages into Northern, Southern (Tuareg), Eastern, and Western varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blažek-2010_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blažek-2010-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Population"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The vast majority of speakers of Berber languages are concentrated in Morocco and Algeria.<sup id="cite_ref-Brenzinger-2007_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brenzinger-2007-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penchoen-1973_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penchoen-1973-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The exact population of speakers has been historically difficult to ascertain due to lack of official recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-Dalby-1998_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalby-1998-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morocco">Morocco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Morocco"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Morocco" title="Languages of Morocco">Languages of Morocco</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png/220px-Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="205" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png/330px-Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png/440px-Percent_of_Berber_speakers_in_Morocco_by_census_2004.png 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="684" /></a><figcaption>Percentage of Berber speakers in Morocco at the 2004 census<sup id="cite_ref-RGPH-2004_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RGPH-2004-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG/220px-Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG" decoding="async" width="220" height="205" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG/330px-Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG/440px-Berber_dialects_in_Morocco.PNG 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="447" /></a><figcaption>Map of Berber-speaking areas in Morocco</figcaption></figure>
<p>Morocco is the country with the greatest number of speakers of Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Brenzinger-2007_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brenzinger-2007-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penchoen-1973_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penchoen-1973-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ennaji-2005_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ennaji-2005-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> As of 2022, Ethnologue estimates there to be 13.8 million speakers of Berber languages in Morocco, based on figures from 2016 and 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Morocco-2022_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morocco-2022-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1960, the first census after Moroccan independence was held. It claimed that 32 percent of Moroccans spoke a Berber language, including bi-, tri- and quadrilingual people.<sup id="cite_ref-Bladi.net-2013_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bladi.net-2013-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2004_Moroccan_census" title="2004 Moroccan census">2004 census</a> found that 3,894,805 Moroccans over five years of age spoke Tashelhit, 2,343,937 spoke Central Atlas Tamazight, and 1,270,986 spoke Tarifit, representing 14.6%, 8.8%, and 4.8% respectively of the surveyed population, or roughly 28.2% of the surveyed population combined.<sup id="cite_ref-RGPH-2004_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RGPH-2004-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2014_Moroccan_census" title="2014 Moroccan census">2014 census</a> found that 14.1% of the population spoke Tashelhit, 7.9% spoke Central Atlas Tamazight, and 4% spoke Tarifit, or about 26% of the population combined.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These estimates, as well as the estimates from various academic sources, are summarized as follows:
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algeria">Algeria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Algeria"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Algeria" title="Languages of Algeria">Languages of Algeria</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg/220px-Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg/330px-Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg/440px-Aires_linguistiques_du_nord-est_alg%C3%A9rien.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1461" data-file-height="881" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shawiya_language" title="Shawiya language">Shawiya</a> languages in the central-eastern part of Algeria</figcaption></figure><p>Algeria is the country with the second greatest number of speakers of Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Brenzinger-2007_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brenzinger-2007-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penchoen-1973_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penchoen-1973-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In 1906, the total population speaking Berber languages in Algeria, excluding the thinly populated Sahara region, was estimated at 1,305,730 out of 4,447,149, or 29%.<sup id="cite_ref-Gautier-1913_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gautier-1913-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Secondary sources disagree on the percentage of self-declared native Berber speakers in the 1966 census, the last Algerian census containing a question about the mother tongue. Some give 17.9%<sup id="cite_ref-Richard_F._Nyrop,_American_University_(Washington,_D.C.)._Foreign_Area_Studies_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_F._Nyrop,_American_University_(Washington,_D.C.)._Foreign_Area_Studies-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Souriau_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Souriau-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lesch_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lesch-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wolfgang_Weissleder_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolfgang_Weissleder-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> while other report 19%.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg/220px-Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg/330px-Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg/440px-Berb%C3%A8res_en_Alg%C3%A9rie_occidentale.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="780" data-file-height="425" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Shenwa_language" title="Shenwa language">Shenwa language</a> in the central-western part of Algeria</figcaption></figure><p><a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a> speakers account for the vast majority of speakers of Berber languages in Algeria. <a href="/wiki/Shawiya_language" title="Shawiya language">Shawiya</a> is the second most commonly spoken Berber language in Algeria. Other Berber languages spoken in Algeria include: <a href="/wiki/Shenwa_language" title="Shenwa language">Shenwa</a>, with 76,300 speakers; Tashelhit, with 6,000 speakers; <a href="/wiki/Ouargli_language" title="Ouargli language">Ouargli</a>, with 20,000 speakers; <a href="/wiki/Tamahaq_language" title="Tamahaq language">Tamahaq</a>, with 71,400 speakers; <a href="/wiki/Tugurt_language" title="Tugurt language">Tugurt</a>, with 8,100 speakers; <a href="/wiki/Tidikelt_language" title="Tidikelt language">Tidikelt</a>, with 1,000 speakers; <a href="/wiki/Gurara_language" title="Gurara language">Gurara</a>, with 11,000 speakers; and <a href="/wiki/Mozabite_language" title="Mozabite language">Mozabite</a>, with 150,000 speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022b_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022b-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Population estimates are summarized as follows:
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_countries">Other countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other countries"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 1998, there were an estimated 450,000 <a href="/wiki/Tawellemmet_language" title="Tawellemmet language">Tawellemmet</a> speakers, 250,000 <a href="/wiki/Air_Tamajeq_language" title="Air Tamajeq language">Air Tamajeq</a> speakers, and 20,000 Tamahaq speakers in <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022c_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022c-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2018 and 2014 respectively, there were an estimated 420,000 speakers of Tawellemmet and 378,000 of Tamasheq in <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022c_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022c-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022e_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022e-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2022, based on figures from 2020, <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a> estimates there to be 285,890 speakers of Berber languages in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>: 247,000 speakers of <a href="/wiki/Nafusi_language" title="Nafusi language">Nafusi</a>, 22,800 speakers of Tamahaq, 13,400 speakers of <a href="/wiki/Ghadam%C3%A8s_language" title="Ghadamès language">Ghadamés</a>, and 2,690 speakers of <a href="/wiki/Awjila_language" title="Awjila language">Awjila</a>. The number of <a href="/wiki/Siwi_language" title="Siwi language">Siwi</a> speakers in Libya is listed as negligible, and the last <a href="/wiki/Sokna_language" title="Sokna language">Sokna</a> speaker is thought to have died in the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022f_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022f-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are an estimated 50,000 <a href="/wiki/Jerba_Berber" title="Jerba Berber">Djerbi</a> speakers in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, based on figures from 2004. <a href="/wiki/Sened_language" title="Sened language">Sened</a> is likely extinct, with the last speaker having died in the 1970s. Ghadamés, though not indigenous to Tunisia, is estimated to have 3,100 speakers throughout the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022g_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022g-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Chenini is one of the rare remaining Berber-speaking villages in Tunisia.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are an estimated 20,000 Siwi speakers in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, based on figures from 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022h_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022h-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2018 and 2017 respectively, there were an estimated 200 speakers of <a href="/wiki/Zenaga_language" title="Zenaga language">Zenaga</a> and 117,000 of <a href="/wiki/Tamasheq_language" title="Tamasheq language">Tamasheq</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022i_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022i-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2009, there were an estimated 122,000 Tamasheq speakers in Burkina Faso.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022j_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022j-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are an estimated 1.5 million speakers of various Berber languages in France.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnologue-2022k_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-2022k-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> A small number of Tawellemmet speakers live in Nigeria.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In total, there are an estimated 3.6 million speakers of Berber languages in countries outside of Morocco and Algeria, summarized as follows:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Status"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After independence, all the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> countries to varying degrees pursued a policy of <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabisation</a>, aimed partly at displacing French from its colonial position as the dominant language of education and literacy. Under this policy the use of the Berber languages was suppressed or even banned. This state of affairs has been contested by Berbers in Morocco and Algeria—especially <a href="/wiki/Kabylie" class="mw-redirect" title="Kabylie">Kabylie</a>—and was addressed in both countries by affording the language official status and introducing it in some schools.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morocco_2">Morocco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Morocco"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Moroccan_Amazigh" title="Standard Moroccan Amazigh">Standard Moroccan Amazigh</a></div>
<p>After gaining independence from France in 1956, Morocco began a period of Arabisation through 1981, with primary and secondary school education gradually being changed to Arabic instruction, and with the aim of having administration done in Arabic, rather than French. During this time, there were riots amongst the Amazigh population, which called for the inclusion of Tamazight as an official language.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2000 Charter for Education Reform marked a change in policy, with its statement of "openness to Tamazight."<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Planning for a public Tamazight-language TV network began in 2006; in 2010, the Moroccan government launched <a href="/wiki/Tamazight_TV" title="Tamazight TV">Tamazight TV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vourlias-2010a2_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vourlias-2010a2-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 29, 2011, Tamazight was added as an official language to the Moroccan constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-Madanin-2012_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madanin-2012-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algeria_2">Algeria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Algeria"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Algerian_Berber" title="Standard Algerian Berber">Standard Algerian Berber</a></div>
<p>After gaining independence from France in 1962, Algeria committed to a policy of Arabisation, which, after 1979, encompassed public education, broadcasting, and the judiciary system. While directed towards the removal of French as an official language, these policies led to dissatisfaction and unrest amongst speakers of Berber languages, who made up about one quarter of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-Bassiouney-2009_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassiouney-2009-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, following riots in Kabylia the previous year, it was announced that Tamazight would be added as a <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a>, though not as an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official</a> one.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This was done on April 8, 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-Bassiouney-2009_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassiouney-2009-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Tamazight has been taught for three hours a week through the first three years of Algerian middle schools since 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Bassiouney-2009_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassiouney-2009-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On January 5, 2016, it was announced that Tamazight had been added as an official language in a draft amendment to the Algerian constitution; it was added to the constitution as an official language on February 7, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_bill-2016_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution_bill-2016-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News-2016_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-2016-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libya">Libya</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Libya"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although regional councils in Libya's <a href="/wiki/Nafusa_Mountains" title="Nafusa Mountains">Nafusa Mountains</a> affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/National_Transitional_Council" title="National Transitional Council">National Transitional Council</a> reportedly use the Berber language of <a href="/wiki/Nafusi_language" title="Nafusi language">Nafusi</a> and have called for it to be granted co-official status with Arabic in a prospective new constitution,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> it does not have official status in Libya as in Morocco and Algeria. As areas of Libya south and west of <a href="/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya" title="Tripoli, Libya">Tripoli</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Nafusa_Mountains" title="Nafusa Mountains">Nafusa Mountains</a> were taken from the control of <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Gaddafi</a> government forces in early summer 2011, Berber workshops and exhibitions sprang up to share and spread the Berber culture and language.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Countries_2">Other Countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other Countries"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Mali and Niger, some <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tuareg languages</a> have been recognized as national languages and have been part of school curriculums since the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Elmedlaoui-2012-2_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elmedlaoui-2012-2-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Berber_language#Phonology" title="Proto-Berber language">Proto-Berber_language §&#160;Phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages#Similarities_in_grammar_and_syntax" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic_languages §&#160;Similarities_in_grammar_and_syntax</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notation">Notation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Notation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In linguistics, the phonology of Berber languages is written with the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">International Phonetic Alphabet</a>, with the following exceptions:<sup id="cite_ref-Elmedlaoui-2012-3_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elmedlaoui-2012-3-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Notation
</th>
<th>Meaning
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>/š/
</td>
<td>unvoiced anterior <a href="/wiki/Postalveolar_consonant" title="Postalveolar consonant">post-alveolar</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>/ž/
</td>
<td>voiced anterior post-alveolar, also in <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>/ɣ/
</td>
<td>voiced uvular fricative (in IPA, this represents the <a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_fricative" title="Voiced velar fricative">voiced velar fricative</a>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>/◌͑/
</td>
<td>voiced pharyngeal fricative
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>/h/
</td>
<td>laryngeal voiced consonant
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>/◌͗/
</td>
<td>glottal stop
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>/ř/
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Strident_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Strident consonant">strident</a> flap or /r̝/, as in <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>!
</td>
<td>indicates the following segment is emphatic
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Consonants"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
The influence of Arabic, the process of <a href="/wiki/Spirantization" class="mw-redirect" title="Spirantization">spirantization</a>, and the absence of labialization have caused the consonant systems of Berber languages to differ significantly by region.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012a_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012a-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Berber languages found north of, and in the northern half of, the Sahara have greater influence from Arabic, including that of loaned phonemes, than those in more southern regions, like Tuareg.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012a_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012a-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Most Berber languages in northern regions have additionally undergone spirantization, in which historical short stops have changed into fricatives.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Northern Berber languages (which is a subset of but not identical to Berber languages in geographically northern regions) commonly have labialized velars and uvulars, unlike other Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm/220px--WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="391" data-durationhint="129" data-mwtitle="WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_(vertical_original).webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_(vertical_original).webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-width="1080" data-height="1920" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4d/WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm.m3u8" type="application/vnd.apple.mpegurl" data-transcodekey="m3u8" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4d/WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="202" data-height="360" /><track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AWIKITONGUES-_Abderrahman_speaking_Tachelhit_%28vertical_original%29.webm&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=vtt&amp;origin=%2A" kind="subtitles" type="text/vtt" srclang="en" label="English ‪(en)‬" data-dir="ltr" /></video></span><figcaption>A video of <a href="/wiki/Shilha_language" title="Shilha language">Tashlhiyt language</a>, one of the Berber languages, spoken by a man from <a href="/wiki/Ait_Melloul" class="mw-redirect" title="Ait Melloul">Ait Melloul</a>.</figcaption></figure><p>Two languages that illustrate the resulting range in consonant inventory across Berber languages are Ahaggar <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tuareg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a>; Kabyle has two more places of articulation and three more manners of articulation than Ahaggar Tuareg.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012a_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012a-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is still, however, common consonant features observed across Berber languages. Almost all Berber languages have bilabial, dental, palatal, velar, uvular, pharyngeal, and laryngeal consonants, and almost all consonants have a long counterpart.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-1997_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-1997-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> All Berber languages, as is common in Afroasiatic languages, have <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealized</a> consonants and phonemic <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">gemination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012a_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012a-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The consonants which may undergo gemination, and the positions in a word where gemination may occur, differ by language.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> They have also been observed to have <a href="/wiki/Tenseness" title="Tenseness">tense</a> and lax consonants, although the status of tense consonants has been the subject of "considerable discussion" by linguists.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-1997_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-1997-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vowel systems of Berber languages also vary widely, with inventories ranging from three phonemic vowels in most <a href="/wiki/Northern_Berber_languages" title="Northern Berber languages">Northern Berber languages</a>, to seven in some <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Berber_languages" title="Eastern Berber languages">Eastern Berber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tuareg</a> languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossman-2012_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossman-2012-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Shilha_language" title="Shilha language">Taselhiyt</a> has the vowels /i/, /a/, and /u/, while Ayer Tuareg has the vowels /i/, /<a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">ə</a>/, /u/, /e/, /<a href="/wiki/Near-open_central_vowel" title="Near-open central vowel">ɐ</a>/, /o/, and /a/.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossman-2012_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossman-2012-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Contrastive <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowel length</a> is rare in Berber languages. Tuareg languages had previously been reported to have contrastive vowel length, but this is no longer the leading analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossman-2012_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossman-2012-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> A complex feature of Berber vowel systems is the role of <a href="/wiki/Central_vowel" title="Central vowel">central vowels</a>, which vary in occurrence and function across languages; there is a debate as to whether <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a> is a proper phoneme of Northern Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suprasegmentals">Suprasegmentals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Suprasegmentals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most Berber languages:
</p>
<ul><li>allow for any combination of CC consonant clusters.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>have no lexical tones.<sup id="cite_ref-Maarten-2012_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maarten-2012-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>either have no lexical stress (Northern Berber languages) or have grammatically significant lexical stress.<sup id="cite_ref-Maarten-2012_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maarten-2012-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetic_correspondences">Phonetic correspondences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Phonetic correspondences"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm/220px--French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="143" data-durationhint="105" data-mwtitle="French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="552" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d9/French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm/French_professor_speaking_fluent_Amazigh.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; <p>Phonetic correspondences between Berber languages are fairly regular.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Some examples, of varying importance and regularity, include [<i>g/ž/y</i>]; [<i>k/š</i>]; [<i>l/ř/r</i>]; [<i>l/ž, ll/dd<sup>ž</sup></i>]; [trill/ vocalized r]; [<i>šš/tt<sup>š</sup></i>]; [<i>ss/tt<sup>š</sup></i>]; [<i>w/g/b</i>]; [<i>q</i>/<i>ɣ</i>]; [<i>h</i>/Ø]; and [<i>s-š-ž/h</i>].<sup id="cite_ref-Elmedlaoui-2012-3_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elmedlaoui-2012-3-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Words in various Berber languages are shown to demonstrate these phonetic correspondences as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Berber languages characteristically make frequent use of <a href="/wiki/Apophony" title="Apophony">apophony</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_ablaut" title="Indo-European ablaut">ablaut</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Berber apophony has been historically analyzed as functioning similarly to the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">Semitic root</a>, but this analysis has fallen out of favor due to the lexical significance of vowels in Berber languages, as opposed to their primarily grammatical significance in Semitic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Part_of_speech" title="Part of speech">lexical categories</a> of all Berber languages are <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adverb" title="Adverb">adverbs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">prepositions</a>. With the exception of a handful of Arabic loanwords in most languages, Berber languages do not have proper adjectives. In Northern and Eastern Berber languages, adjectives are a subcatergory of nouns; in Tuareg, relative clauses and stative verb forms are used to modify nouns instead.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012f_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012f-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The gender, number, and case of nouns, as well as the gender, number, and person of verbs, are typically distinguished through affixes.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007-2_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-2-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012c_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012c-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Arguments are described with word order and <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012d_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012d-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> When sentences have a verb, they essentially follow <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object_word_order" title="Verb–subject–object word order">verb–subject–object word order</a>, although some linguists believe alternate descriptors would better categorize certain languages, such as Taqbaylit.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mettouchi-2010_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mettouchi-2010-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">Pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Berber languages have both independent and dependent pronouns, both of which distinguish between person and number. Gender is also typically distinguished in the second and third person, and sometimes in first person plural.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012d_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012d-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguist <a href="/wiki/Maarten_Kossmann" title="Maarten Kossmann">Maarten Kossmann</a> divides pronouns in Berber languages into three morphological groups:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012d_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012d-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Independent pronouns</li>
<li>Direct object clitics</li>
<li>Indirect object clitics; prepositional suffixes; adnominal suffixes</li></ol>
<p>When clitics precede or follow a verb, they are almost always ordered with the indirect object first, direct object second, and <a href="/wiki/Andative_and_venitive" title="Andative and venitive">andative-venitive</a> <a href="/wiki/Deixis" title="Deixis">deictic</a> clitic last. An example in Tarifit is shown as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012d_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012d-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">y-əwš=as=θ=ið</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3SG</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="masculine gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">M</abbr>-give:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="past tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PAST</abbr>=<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3SG</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="indirect object" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">IO</abbr>=<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3SG</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="masculine gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">M</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="direct object" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">DO</abbr>=<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="venitive (coming towards)" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">VEN</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">y-əwš=as=θ=ið</p><p style="display: none;">3SG:M-give:PAST=3SG:IO=3SG:M:DO=VEN</p><p style="clear: left;">"He gave it to him (in this direction)." (Tarifit)</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>The allowed positioning of different kinds of clitics varies by language.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012d_122-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012d-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">Nouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nouns are distinguished by <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, and case in most Berber languages, with gender being feminine or masculine, number being singular or plural, and case being in the construct or free state.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007-2_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-2-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gender can be feminine or masculine, and can be lexically determined, or can be used to distinguish qualities of the noun.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> For humans and "higher" animals (such as mammals and large birds), gender distinguishes sex, whereas for objects and "lesser" animals (such as insects and lizards), it distinguishes size. For some nouns, often fruits and vegetables, gender can also distinguish the specificity of the noun.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012e_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012e-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The ways in which gender is used to distinguish nouns is shown in as follows, with examples from <a href="/wiki/Figuig_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Figuig language">Figuig</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012e_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012e-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>

</th>
