<td><i>a-ɣənža</i>
</td>
<td>"large spoon"
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2">varies, but typically fruits and vegetables
</td>
<td rowspan="2">unit noun
</td>
<td><i>ta-mlul-t</i>
</td>
<td>"(one) melon"
</td>
<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Collective_noun" title="Collective noun">collective noun</a>
</td>
<td rowspan="2"><i>a-mlul</i>
</td>
<td rowspan="2">"melons (in general)"
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><i>ti-mlal</i> (plural)
</td>
<td>"(specific) melons"
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An example of nouns with lexically determined gender are the feminine <i>t-lussi</i> ("butter") and masculine <i>a-ɣi</i> ("buttermilk") in Figuig.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007_118-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Mass nouns have lexically determined gender across Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012e_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012e-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Berber languages have two cases, which distinguish the <a href="/wiki/Construct_state" title="Construct state">construct state</a> from the free state.<sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007c_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007c-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The construct state is also called the "construct case, "relative case," "annexed state" (<i>état d'annexion</i>)<i>,</i> or the "nominative case"; the free state (<i>état libre</i>) is also called the "direct case" or "accusative case."<sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> When present, case is always expressed through nominal prefixes and initial-vowel reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007c_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007c-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The use of the <a href="/wiki/Marked_nominative" class="mw-redirect" title="Marked nominative">marked nominative</a> system and constructions similar to <a href="/wiki/Active-stative_alignment" class="mw-redirect" title="Active-stative alignment">Split-S alignment</a> varies by language.<sup id="cite_ref-Mettouchi-2010_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mettouchi-2010-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Eastern Berber languages do not have case.<sup id="cite_ref-König-2008_55-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König-2008-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007c_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007c-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Number can be singular or plural, which is marked with prefixation, suffixation, and sometimes apophony. Nouns usually are made plural by one of either suffixation or apophony, with prefixation applied independently. Specifics vary by language, but prefixation typically changes singular <i>a-</i> and <i>ta-</i> to plural <i>i-</i> and <i>ti-</i> respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007-2_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007-2-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The number of mass nouns are lexically determined. For example, in multiple Berber languages, such as Figuig, <i>a-ɣi</i> ("buttermilk") is singular while <i>am-an</i> ("water") is plural.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012e_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012e-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nouns or pronouns — optionally extended with genitival pronominal affixes, demonstrative clitics, or pre-nominal elements, and then further modified by numerals, adjectives, possessive phrases, or relative clauses — can be built into noun phrases.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007f_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007f-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Possessive phrases in noun phrases must have a genitive proposition.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012f_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012f-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007f_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007f-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a limited number of pre-nominal elements, which function similarly to pronoun syntactic heads of the noun phrase, and which can be categorized into three types as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007f_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007f-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The pluralizer <i>id-</i></li>
<li>The four pre-nominal elements roughly meaning "son(s) of" and "daughter(s) of", which commonly denote group identity and origin</li>
<li>Pre-nominal elements which expand on the meaning of the noun</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Verbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Verb bases are formed by stems that are optionally extended by prefixes, with mood, aspect, and negation applied with a vocalic scheme. This form can then be conjugated with affixes to agree with person, number, and gender, which produces a word.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012c_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012c-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Different linguists analyze and label <a href="/wiki/Aspect_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspect (linguistics)">aspects</a> in the Berber languages very differently. Kossman roughly summarizes the basic stems which denote aspect as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007e_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007e-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aorist" title="Aorist">Aorist</a>, also called aoriste, without a preceding particle:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative</a></li>
<li>unmarked (taking aspect from preceding verb)</li></ul></li>
<li>Aorist, with the preceding article <i>ad:</i>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Irrealis_mood" title="Irrealis mood">irrealis</a>  (adhortative, future)</li></ul></li>
<li>Preterite, or accompli:
<ul><li>past tense, in dynamic use</li>
<li>states (such as "to want, to know"), in stative use</li></ul></li>
<li>Intensive Aorist, also called habitative or inaccompli:
<ul><li>dynamic present</li>
<li>habitative and iterative</li>
<li>habitative imperative</li>
<li>negation of any imperative</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Different languages may have more stems and aspects, or may distinguish within the above categories. Stem formation can be very complex, with Tuareg by some measures having over two hundred identified conjugation subtypes.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007e_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007e-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The aspectual stems of some classes of verbs in various Berber languages are shown as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>Verb phrases are built with verb morphology, pronominal and deictic clitics, pre-verbal particles, and auxiliary elements. The pre-verbal particles are <i>ad</i>, <i>wər,</i> and their variants, which correspond to the meanings of "non-realized" and "negative" respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerals">Numerals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Numerals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many Berber languages have lost use of their original numerals from three onwards due to the influence of Arabic; Tarifit has lost all except one. Languages that may retain all their original numerals include Tashelhiyt, Tuareg, <a href="/wiki/Ghadam%C3%A8s_language" title="Ghadamès language">Ghadames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ouargli_language" title="Ouargli language">Ouargla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zenaga_language" title="Zenaga language">Zenaga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Corpus-2015_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corpus-2015-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012b_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012b-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Original Berber numerals agree in gender with the noun they describe, whereas the borrowed Arabic forms do not.<sup id="cite_ref-Corpus-2015_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corpus-2015-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012b_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012b-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The numerals 1–10 in Tashelhiyt and Mali Tuareg are as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2012b_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2012b-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sentence_structure">Sentence structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sentence structure"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sentences in Berber languages can be divided into verbal and non-verbal sentences. The <a href="/wiki/Topic_and_comment" title="Topic and comment">topic</a>, which has a unique intonation in the sentence, precedes all other arguments in both types.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Verbal sentences have a finite verb, and are commonly understood to follow <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object_word_order" title="Verb–subject–object word order">verb–subject–object word order</a> (VSO).<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mettouchi-2010_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mettouchi-2010-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Some linguists have proposed opposing analyses of the word order patterns in Berber languages, and there has been some support for characterizing Taqbaylit as <a href="/wiki/Non-configurational_language" title="Non-configurational language">discourse-configurational</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mettouchi-2010_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mettouchi-2010-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Existential, attributive, and locational sentences in most Berber languages are expressed with a non-verbal sentence, which have no finite verb. In these sentences, the predicate follows the noun, with the predicative particle <i>d</i> sometimes in between. Two examples, one without and one with a subject, are given from Kabyle as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ð</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="predicate/predicative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PRED</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">a-qšiš</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="free state, also called the accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">EL</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="masculine gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">M</abbr>-boy</p></div><p style="display: none;">ð a-qšiš</p><p style="display: none;">PRED <abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="free state, also called the accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">EL</abbr>:M-boy</p><p style="clear: left;">"It is a boy." (Kabyle)</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">nətta</p><p style="margin: 0px;">he</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ð</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="predicate/predicative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PRED</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">a-qšiš</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="free state, also called the accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">EL</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="masculine gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">M</abbr>-boy</p></div><p style="display: none;">nətta ð a-qšiš</p><p style="display: none;">he PRED <abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="free state, also called the accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">EL</abbr>:M-boy</p><p style="clear: left;">"He is a boy." (Kabyle)</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div><p>Non-verbal sentences may use the verb meaning "to be," which exists in all Berber languages. An example from Tarifit is given as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2007d_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2007d-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:2em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">i-tiři</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="third person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">3SG</abbr>:<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="masculine gender" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">M</abbr>-be:<abbr class="gloss-abbr gloss-abbr-ambiguous" title="imperfective" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">I</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ða</p><p style="margin: 0px;">here</p></div><p style="display: none;">i-tiři ða</p><p style="display: none;">3SG:M-be:<abbr class="gloss-abbr gloss-abbr-ambiguous" title="imperfective" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">I</abbr> here</p><p style="clear: left;">"He is always here." (habitual) (Tarifit)</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lexicon">Lexicon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Lexicon"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Above all in the area of basic lexicon, the Berber languages are very similar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, the household-related vocabulary in sedentary tribes is especially different from the one found in nomadic ones, whereas Tahaggart has only two or three designations for species of palm tree, other languages may have as many as 200 similar words.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, Tahaggart has a rich vocabulary for the description of camels.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some loanwords in the Berber languages can be traced to pre-Roman times. The Berber words <i>te-ḇăyne</i> "date" and <i>a-sḇan</i> "loose woody tissue around the palm tree stem" originate from <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Ancient Egyptian</a>, likely due to the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> cultivation into North Africa from Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Around a dozen Berber words are probable <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician-Punic</a> loanwords, although the overall influence of Phoenician-Punic on Berber languages is negligible.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> A number of loanwords could be attributed to Phoenician-Punic, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>. The similar vocabulary between these Semitic languages, as well as Arabic, is a complicating factor in tracing the etymology of certain words.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Words of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> origin have been introduced into Berber languages over time. Maarten Kossman separates Latin loanwords in Berber languages into those from during the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman empire</a> ("Latin loans"), from after the fall of the Roman empire ("<a href="/wiki/African_Romance" title="African Romance">African Romance</a> loans"), precolonial non-African Romance loans, and colonial and post-colonial Romance loans. It can be difficult to distinguish Latin from African Romance loans.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013b_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013b-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> There are about 40 likely Latin or African Romance loanwords in Berber languages, which tend to be agricultural terms, religious terms, terms related to learning, or words for plants or useful objects.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013b_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013b-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013c_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013c-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Use of these terms varies by language. For example, Tuareg does not retain the Latin agricultural terms, which relate to a form of agriculture not practiced by the Tuareg people. There are some Latin loans that are only known to be used in Shawiya.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013c_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013c-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Berber_calendar" title="Berber calendar">Berber calendar</a> uses month names derived from the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>. Not every language uses every month. For example, Figuig appears to use only eight of the months.<sup id="cite_ref-Kossmann-2013c_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kossmann-2013c-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> These names may be precolonial non-African Romance loans, adopted into Berber languages through Arabic, rather than from Latin directly.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most influential external language on the lexicon of Berber languages is Arabic. Maarten Kossmann calculates that 0-5% of Ghadames and Awdjila's core vocabularies, and over 15% of Ghomara, Siwa, and Senhadja de Sraïr's core vocabularies, are loans from Arabic. Most other Berber languages loan from 6–15% of their core vocabulary from Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Salem_Chaker" title="Salem Chaker">Salem Chaker</a> estimates that Arabic loanwords represent 38% of <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a> vocabulary, 25% of Tashelhiyt vocabulary, and 5% of Tuareg vocabulary, including non-core words.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the one hand, the words and expressions connected to Islam were borrowed, e.g. Tashlhiyt <i>bismillah</i> "in the name of <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>" &lt; Classical Arabic <i>bi-smi-llāhi</i>, Tuareg <i>ta-mejjīda</i> "mosque" (Arabic <i>masjid</i>); on the other, Berber adopted cultural concepts such as Kabyle <i>ssuq</i> "market" from Arabic <i>as-sūq</i>, <i>tamdint</i> "town" &lt; Arabic <i>madīna</i>. Even expressions such as the Arabic greeting <i>as-salāmu ʿalaikum</i> "Peace be upon you!" were adopted (Tuareg <i>salāmu ɣlīkum</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The Berber languages often have original Berber designations besides the Arabic loans; for instance, both the inherited word <i>ataram</i> and the loan <i>lɣərb</i> (Arabic <i>al-ġarb</i>) coexist in Kabyle.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_other_languages">Influence on other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Influence on other languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Berber languages have influenced local <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> dialects in the Maghreb. Although <a href="/wiki/Maghrebi_Arabic" title="Maghrebi Arabic">Maghrebi Arabic</a> has a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> vocabulary,<sup id="cite_ref-Abdou_Elimam2_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdou_Elimam2-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> it contains a few Berber loanwords which represent 2–3% of the vocabulary of <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Arabic" title="Libyan Arabic">Libyan Arabic</a>, 8–9% of <a href="/wiki/Algerian_Arabic" title="Algerian Arabic">Algerian Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Arabic" title="Tunisian Arabic">Tunisian Arabic</a>, and 10–15% of <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_Arabic" title="Moroccan Arabic">Moroccan Arabic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:022_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:022-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Their influence is also seen in some languages in West Africa. F. W. H. Migeod pointed to strong resemblances between Berber and <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a> in such words and phrases as these: Berber: <b>obanis</b>; Hausa <i>obansa</i> (his father); Berber: <b>a bat</b>; Hausa <i>ya bata</i> (he was lost); Berber: <b>eghare</b>; Hausa <i>ya kirra</i> (he called). In addition he notes that the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> in both languages is formed with <b>n</b> = "of".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinct_languages">Extinct languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Berber_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Extinct languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A number of extinct populations are believed to have spoken Afro-Asiatic languages of the Berber branch. According to Peter Behrens (1981) and Marianne Bechaus-Gerst (2000), linguistic evidence suggests that the peoples of the <a href="/wiki/C-Group_culture" title="C-Group culture">C-Group culture</a> in present-day southern <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> spoke Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Bechaus-Gerst_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bechaus-Gerst-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lbant_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lbant-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a> <a href="/wiki/Nobiin_language" title="Nobiin language">Nobiin language</a> today contains a number of key pastoralism related <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> that are of Berber origin, including the terms for sheep and water/<a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. This in turn suggests that the C-Group population—which, along with the <a href="/wiki/Kerma_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerma Culture">Kerma culture</a>, inhabited the Nile valley immediately before the arrival of the first <a href="/wiki/Nubian_languages" title="Nubian languages">Nubian</a> speakers—spoke Afro-Asiatic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-Bechaus-Gerst_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bechaus-Gerst-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, historical linguistics indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Guanche_language" title="Guanche language">Guanche language</a>, which was spoken on the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Guanches" title="Guanches">Guanches</a>, likely belonged to the Berber branch of the Afro-Asiatic family.<sup id="cite_ref-Hawyard_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hawyard-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Brett-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Brett_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFBrett2023" class="citation encyclopaedia cs1">Brett, Michael (23 May 2023). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Berber">"Berber"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Britannica" class="mw-redirect" title="Britannica">Britannica</a>.com</i>. <q>By <i>[the 14th century]</i>, however, the Berbers were in retreat, subjected to Arabization of two very different kinds. The predominance of written Arabic had ended the writing of Amazigh (Berber) languages in both the old Libyan and the new Arabic script, reducing its languages to folk languages.</q> &#91;In other words, Tamazight had earlier been the dominant spoken <i>and</i> written language of the Imazighen.&#93;</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Berber&amp;rft.btitle=Britannica.com&amp;rft.date=2023-05-23&amp;rft.aulast=Brett&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2Ftopic%2FBerber&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Campbell-2012b-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Campbell-2012b_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFCampbell2012" class="citation book cs1">Campbell, George L. (2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/810078009"><i>The Routledge handbook of scripts and alphabets</i></a>. Christopher Moseley (2nd&#160;ed.). Milton Park, Abingdon, Oxon: Routledge. pp.&#160;58–59. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-203-86548-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-203-86548-4"><bdi>978-0-203-86548-4</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/810078009">810078009</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Routledge+handbook+of+scripts+and+alphabets&amp;rft.place=Milton+Park%2C+Abingdon%2C+Oxon&amp;rft.pages=58-59&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F810078009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-203-86548-4&amp;rft.aulast=Campbell&amp;rft.aufirst=George+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.worldcat.org%2Foclc%2F810078009&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFBriggs1957" class="citation journal cs1">Briggs, L. Cabot (February 1957). "A Review of the Physical Anthropology of the Sahara and Its Prehistoric Implications". <i>Man</i>. <b>56</b>: 20–23. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F2793877">10.2307/2793877</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/2793877">2793877</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Man&amp;rft.atitle=A+Review+of+the+Physical+Anthropology+of+the+Sahara+and+Its+Prehistoric+Implications&amp;rft.volume=56&amp;rft.pages=20-23&amp;rft.date=1957-02&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F2793877&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F2793877%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.aulast=Briggs&amp;rft.aufirst=L.+Cabot&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Punic-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Punic_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/Libyco-Berber_alphabet" title="Libyco-Berber alphabet">Libyco-Berber alphabet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tifinagh" title="Tifinagh">Tifinagh</a>: "The word tifinagh (singular tafinəq &lt; *ta-finəɣ-t) is thought by some scholars to be a Berberized feminine plural cognate or adaptation of the Latin word 'Punicus', (meaning 'Punic' or 'Phoenician') through the Berber feminine prefix ti- and the root <b>√</b>FNƔ &lt; *<b>√</b>PNQ &lt; Latin Punicus; thus tifinagh could possibly mean 'the Phoenician (letters)' or 'the Punic letters'."<br />The adjective "Punic" commonly refers to <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, destroyed by Rome at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>, 146 BCE. In the usual theory, Carthaginians were western Phoenicians. But maybe not so much:<br />•&#160;<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFMoots2022" class="citation journal cs1">Moots, Hannah M.; et&#160;al. (13 March 2022). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/2022.03.13.483276v1.full.pdf">"A Genetic History of Continuity and Mobility in the Iron Age Central Mediterranean"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Biorxiv.org</i>. Stanford, California. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2F2022.03.13.483276">10.1101/2022.03.13.483276</a>. <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:247549249">247549249</a>. <q>The contribution of autochthonous North African populations in Carthaginian history is obscured by the use of terms like 'Western Phoenicians', and even to an extent, 'Punic', in the literature to refer to Carthaginians, as it implies a primarily colonial population and diminishes indigenous involvement in the Carthaginian Empire. As a result, the role of autochthonous populations has been largely overlooked in studies of Carthage and its empire. Genetic approaches are well suited to examine such assumptions, and here we show that North African populations contributed substantially to the genetic makeup of Carthaginian cities.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Biorxiv.org&amp;rft.atitle=A+Genetic+History+of+Continuity+and+Mobility+in+the+Iron+Age+Central+Mediterranean&amp;rft.date=2022-03-13&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1101%2F2022.03.13.483276&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A247549249%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Moots&amp;rft.aufirst=Hannah+M.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.biorxiv.org%2Fcontent%2F10.1101%2F2022.03.13.483276v1.full.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFSoulaimani2016" class="citation journal cs1">Soulaimani, Dris (2 January 2016). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1080/17586801.2015.1023176">"Writing and rewriting Amazigh/Berber identity: Orthographies and language ideologies"</a>. <i>Writing Systems Research</i>. <b>8</b> (1): 2–5. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1080%2F17586801.2015.1023176">10.1080/17586801.2015.1023176</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/issn/1758-6801">1758-6801</a>. <a href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:144700140">144700140</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Writing+Systems+Research&amp;rft.atitle=Writing+and+rewriting+Amazigh%2FBerber+identity%3A+Orthographies+and+language+ideologies&amp;rft.volume=8&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=2-5&amp;rft.date=2016-01-02&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A144700140%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.issn=1758-6801&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1080%2F17586801.2015.1023176&amp;rft.aulast=Soulaimani&amp;rft.aufirst=Dris&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1080%2F17586801.2015.1023176&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Gautier-1913-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Gautier-1913_68-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Gautier-1913_68-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFGautier1913" class="citation journal cs1">Gautier, É.-F. (1913). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/23437471">"Répartition de la Langue Berbère en Algérie"</a>. <i>Annales de Géographie</i>. <b>22</b> (123): 256. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3406%2Fgeo.1913.8304">10.3406/geo.1913.8304</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/issn/0003-4010">0003-4010</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/23437471">23437471</a>. <q>Les chiffres se rapportent, non pas au dernier recensement, celui de 1911, mais au précédenl, celui de 1906. C'est le seul sur lequel on avait, et même on a encore maintenant, des données suffisantes. Voici ces chiffres. Sur une population indigène totale de 4 447 149 hab., nous trouvons 1 305 730 berbérophones; c'est un peu moins du tiers.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annales+de+G%C3%A9ographie&amp;rft.atitle=R%C3%A9partition+de+la+Langue+Berb%C3%A8re+en+Alg%C3%A9rie&amp;rft.volume=22&amp;rft.issue=123&amp;rft.pages=256&amp;rft.date=1913&amp;rft.issn=0003-4010&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F23437471%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3406%2Fgeo.1913.8304&amp;rft.aulast=Gautier&amp;rft.aufirst=%C3%89.-F.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F23437471&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>

State University of New York Press. p.&#160;174. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4384-2393-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4384-2393-7"><bdi>978-1-4384-2393-7</bdi></a>. <q>Zavadovskij gives statistics for the percentage of Berber words in North African Muslim Arabic dialects: 10–15 percent Berber components in the Moroccan Arabic lexicon, 8–9 percent in Algerian and Tunisian Arabic, and only 2–3 percent in Libyan Arabic.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Non-Jewish+Origins+of+the+Sephardic+Jews&amp;rft.pages=174&amp;rft.pub=State+University+of+New+York+Press&amp;rft.date=2012-02-01&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4384-2393-7&amp;rft.aulast=Wexler&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DYJpdiPiG2g4C%26pg%3DPA174&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABerber+languages" class="Z3988"></span></span>
